  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_1e34e826-81cf-4b6e-a106-51ab9a83609f'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

Detective story ever..<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492881273i/22563683._SY540_.jpg" width="450" height="450" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Or his delving into the matter, death, soul, mind and even God and free well by hypothesis.<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493056805i/22579281._SY540_.jpg" width="450" height="450" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>Or of course, the delving into the man's Conscience, guilt and self-destruction..<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493841350i/22658271._SY540_.jpg" width="450" height="609" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>There's even kind of Romance, Fantasy, adventure, treasure hunt, code breaking, satire...<br><br>He inspired <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3137322.Fyodor_Dostoyevsky" title="Fyodor Dostoyevsky" rel="nofollow noopener">Fyodor Dostoyevsky</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2448.Arthur_Conan_Doyle" title="Arthur Conan Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">Arthur Conan Doyle</a> with crime novels,<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492881273i/22563686._SX540_.jpg" width="450" height="132" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And inspired <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/696805.Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne" rel="nofollow noopener">Jules Verne</a> ,then <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/15669786.H_G_Well" title="H G Well" rel="nofollow noopener">H G Wells</a> and even recently <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6540057.Andy_Weir" title="Andy Weir" rel="nofollow noopener">Andy Weir</a> with his science fiction stories,<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1492279956i/22501775._SX540_.png" width="500" height="378" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And so more... just by his wild imagination..<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493151304i/22587876._SY540_.jpg" width="450" height="656" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So the five stars here are for his originality and innovations in stories..<br>Which is not just little Horror Stories..<br><br>~~~~~~~~~~~~~~<br>Personal Note:<br>----------<br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1493151304i/22587872._SX540_.jpg" width="500" height="375" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>In order to enjoy his stories, I read first the Arabic -complete, unbridged- translation of it... then reviewing and fast readin it in its original forum, since Poe use a bit hard language for me and he's got a way in 'digression' sometimes and complex sentences.<br><br>So I skipped few stories here till I have the translation of it-and even enjoyed some stories that aren't in this edition-, so I'll be back to this book again soon.<br><br>Mohammed Arabey<br>From 20 April 2017<br>To 6 May 2017</span>
  <a data-text-id="13008222578486815516" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_1993218435" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1993218435" />
    <a title="A. Dawes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/55066255-a-dawes"><img alt="A. Dawes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1470731545p2/55066255.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1993218435?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="A. Dawes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="A. Dawes" href="/user/show/55066255-a-dawes">A. Dawes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007420223" href="/book/show/12410969-tales-of-mystery-and-imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/55066255-a-dawes?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1993218435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8394933494983130086">This will be another ongoing review, as I've been doing with collections and anthologies. Poe has always mesmerised me in terms of his influence on writers and the short story genre at large; his poetry is also both haunting and relatively accessible. This collection was given to me in hardback, 31 years ago. Its pages are now yellow and aged, but the contents within are better than I remember. If you're patient with Poe, I think you'll find he's very rewarding in return. If you rush through and</span>
  <span id="freeText8394933494983130086" style="display:none">This will be another ongoing review, as I've been doing with collections and anthologies. Poe has always mesmerised me in terms of his influence on writers and the short story genre at large; his poetry is also both haunting and relatively accessible. This collection was given to me in hardback, 31 years ago. Its pages are now yellow and aged, but the contents within are better than I remember. If you're patient with Poe, I think you'll find he's very rewarding in return. If you rush through and don't enjoy the rhythm of the prose, I'd imagine he'd be tiresome. He's one of those writers to be savoured when you have the time rather than a page turning entertainment.<br /><br />William Wilson 5* William Wilson" is all about 'the double'. Even the eponymous title is a pun of sorts: Will-I-am, and Wil-son. Through a doppelganger haunting William, Poe explores the concept of sanity. An interesting story with long sentences but little specific imagery. Psychologically chilling. <br /><br />The Gold-Bug 3* A little more dated, involving a bug bite (and potential madness) and a cryptogram. Not a storyline that did much for me personally. <br /><br />The Fall of the House of Usher 5* A classic Poe story. The unnamed narrator visits the crumbling gothic mansion of his friend Usher to learn of Usher's sister's death. But not all is as seems. This story is the very opposite of William Wilson in terms of style. Rather than being devoid of imagery, Poe illustrates his stylistic variation and prowess with a story of sensory imagery overload. Poe manages this detailed gothic prose to build on an especially macabre mood and atmosphere. The language aesthetics here are as powerful as the story. A gothic horror masterpiece. <br /><br />The Masque of Red Death 5* Another gothic fantasy. Holed up in an aristocratic castle trying to avoid 'the Red Death'- basically the plague; a masquerade ball takes a catastrophic turn. A masked guest in blue is the Red Death personified. And although deadly, he is both more and less than he seems. Foreboding atmosphere throughout. A strong tale.<br /><br />The Cask of Amontillado 4.5* Like most of Poe's stories, this haunted me long after I finished it. What atmosphere could be more chilling than a man murdering another for revenge at Carnivale time in a unnamed part of Italy? The protagonist, Montresor, somehow holds Fortunato accountable for his own decline in fortunes. Fortunato (obviously relating to fortune) is a happy, respected and an admired member of society, who has risen through the ranks, possibly with the aid of The Freemasons. He is everything Montresor once was, and he has apparently made a few derogatory comments about Montresor, although whether this is the reason for his murder, or whether the reason is an envy bordering on lunacy, is questionable. Another excellent dark story. <br /><br />A Descent into the Maelstrom 3* An old-fashioned tale of a maelstrom whirlpool survivor. Only Poe takes the wonders and beauty of the vortex further. Not my personal favourite, but I'd think a few sci-fi fans would love it.<br /><br />The Pit and the Pendulum 4* As a reader who enjoys dark fiction, fantasy and historical fiction, this imaginative tale of torture during the Spanish Inquisition really intrigued me. The strong aural imagery throughout takes us almost into the realm of the ghostly too. I feel as though this story had a great influence on gothic horror tales in general. While not as complicated as some other stories here, it's still a captivating narrative.<br /><br />The Purloined Letter 3.5* A forerunner for short detective fiction. A letter has been stolen and there appears blackmail (and more) at hand. There are far better detective stories out there, but this is an interesting look at an early exponent of the genre. <br /><br />Metzengerstein 4* A gothic tale on a hyperbolic scale. This one is devoid of subtlety and is rather heavy handed with regards to both the gothic tropes along with its overt symbolism. In this tale, Frederick, the sole living member of the Metzengerstein family line, has a long standing vendetta with the Berlifitzing clan. When a fire destroys the Berlifitzing patriarch, Frederick is suspected. Frederick, however, is obsessed with a wild horse, which has eerily gone unobserved. Metzengerstein's own home is then set alight, and with an ironic justice, the wild horse, with Frederick on it, charges into the flames. A clever case of guilt personified -or guilt hippofied...<br /><br />The Murders in the Rue Morgue 3.5* Murders, balconies, and that good ol'orangutan... Detective Dupin from The Purloined Letter is back again. Once again this mystery story is not nearly as sharp as the detective stories of today. In fact, this story is laughable. I still liked it though, as I don't think I'll ever read another mystery story involving a Bornean great ape.<br /><br />The Tell-tale Heart 5* One of the most famous of Poe's stories, so it doesn't require any of my clumsy commentary. Lovely way to end the collection.<br /><br />This collection is worth it. It chiefly contains the best of Poe with a range of styles on display, and those few tales that aren't his best are still incredibly interesting in terms of the evolution of the short story. When on song, Poe is a gothic master, and the chilling psychology behind most of these haunting tales adds extra layers to the intrigue and uncertainty, and these layers exemplify Poe's greatest works.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8394933494983130086" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1005315307" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1005315307" />
    <a title="Warwick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53165-warwick"><img alt="Warwick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1510406793p2/53165.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1005315307?book_show_action=true">Jul 24, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Warwick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Warwick" href="/user/show/53165-warwick">Warwick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/10620556-tales-of-mystery-and-imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=folio-society">folio-society</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=gothic">gothic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/53165-warwick?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1005315307" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9832553557793744787"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406228367i/10522512._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>I remember visiting the Edgar Allan Poe museum the last time I was in Richmond, Virginia. At the time I don't think I had read any of his work, except perhaps The Raven. The museum was a creepy place, as you might imagine, with a lot of dark wood and eerie pictures and a strange garden that seemed to be in permanent shadow. It was a strange place and he was a strange man – a hard writer to pin down: distinctly American, but hugely influential in European letters; not technically a very brilliant</span>
  <span id="freeText9832553557793744787" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406228367i/10522512._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I remember visiting the Edgar Allan Poe museum the last time I was in Richmond, Virginia. At the time I don't think I had read any of his work, except perhaps The Raven. The museum was a creepy place, as you might imagine, with a lot of dark wood and eerie pictures and a strange garden that seemed to be in permanent shadow. It was a strange place and he was a strange man – a hard writer to pin down: distinctly American, but hugely influential in European letters; not technically a very brilliant writer, and yet the founder of half a dozen new literary genres.<br><br>Reading him feels, to me, like an act of almost shameful self-indulgence; rich but sickly; you feel you need a brisk walk afterwards. His weird stories mark a bridge between the Gothic and the new movements of symbolism and decadence and, later, the genres that would become known as horror and science fiction. He also invented the modern detective story.<br><br>I think of him as one of those writers that translates easily. In the same way, Tolstoy is venerated by non-Russians while native speakers find his prose mediocre. French speakers often say something similar about Victor Hugo. And the French were, it must be said, quite obsessed with ‘Edgar Poe’, particularly after his works were translated by Baudelaire.<br><br><i>Quelque chose de monomanique</i> was the shrewd judgement of the Goncourts. Hard to argue with that. The predominant theme is death, but death elevated to a supernatural vividness and importance. The archetypal image of his works, for me, is the image of the young, beautiful, dead woman. This trope features heavily in ‘Morella’, ‘Berenice’, ‘The Fall of the House of Usher’, ‘Ligeia’ – and indeed in Poe's own life, because he married his thirteen-year-old cousin and she went on to die of tuberculosis when she was twenty-four. The death clearly left a lasting imprint on him.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406228553i/10522539._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>So, yes: thanatophilia. I'm rolling out the long words. But it's true. Have a look at how he chooses to end ‘The Masque of the Red Death’, for instance:<br><br><blockquote>And now was acknowledged the presence of the Red Death. He had come like a thief in the night. And one by one dropped the revellers in the blood-bedewed halls of their revel, and died each in the despairing posture of his fall. And the life of the ebony clock went out with that of the last of the gay. And the flames of the tripods expired. And Darkness and Decay and the Red Death held illimitable dominion over all.</blockquote><br><br>Sleep tight, kids! Another story ends: ‘the grave was still a home, and the corrosive hours, co-mates.’ Another ends: ‘there lay a nearly liquid mass of loathsome—of detestable putridity.’ Another ends – well you get the idea.<br><br>Poe's prose is melodramatic and rococo and makes full use of Grand Exclamations! And <i>italicised phrases of dread</i>! Oh the Horror and the Agony! And nothing but the drear grave and the worm for evermore! And so forth. But he is also imaginative and, sometimes, positively economical, setting the scene brilliantly in just a few short sentences and creating an atmosphere all his own (what Allen Ginsberg called his ‘demonic dreaminess’). His vocabulary, steeped as it is in the high-flown tradition of dark romanticism, was a constant delight to me, built of ornate items like <i>sulphureous</i>, <i>pulsation</i>, <i>exergue</i>, <i>faucial</i>, <i>chasmal</i>, <i>cachinnatory</i>, <i>asphyctic</i> and many more goodies besides.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1406228367i/10522513._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Jorge Luis Borges said that Poe's writings as a whole constitute a work of genius, although each individual piece is flawed. This is a very appealing assessment. He is an important writer, and often a very fascinating and enjoyable one – but that said, I don't really feel the desire to spend all that much time in his company.<br><br>However, make sure you get a version with Harry Clarke's angular, Beardsley-esque illustrations. They are superlative.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9832553557793744787" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_580894962" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/580894962" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/580894962?book_show_action=true">Apr 04, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781853260131" href="/book/show/263244.Tales_of_Mystery_and_Imagination">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">lovers of disturbing stories</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      Emilie
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2013">read-in-2013</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer580894962" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer858994087343896168"><i>"An artist is usually a damned liar, but his art, if it be art, will tell you the truth of his day." </i> D.H. Lawrence.<br/><br/>Burying people alive, ghosts, macabre deaths of usually delicate and young women, dark magic, effects of inebriation and hallucination, torture, whirlpools sucking people out of their time, fatal plagues, abnormal psychological states, obsessional behaviors... William Blake in prose.<br/>If D.H. Lawrence was any close to right about his predicament I wouldn't have liked to be in Mr.Poe'</span>
  <span id="freeText858994087343896168" style="display:none"><i>"An artist is usually a damned liar, but his art, if it be art, will tell you the truth of his day." </i> D.H. Lawrence.<br /><br />Burying people alive, ghosts, macabre deaths of usually delicate and young women, dark magic, effects of inebriation and hallucination, torture, whirlpools sucking people out of their time, fatal plagues, abnormal psychological states, obsessional behaviors... William Blake in prose.<br />If D.H. Lawrence was any close to right about his predicament I wouldn't have liked to be in Mr.Poe's skin, such horrors!<br />That Poe lead a tormented and dysfunctional life is no secret. Haunted by the death of his mother when he was barely a toddler and later by the long illness and ultimate death of the love of his life(his cousin Virginia)whom he married when she was only thirteen, Poe struggled to keep afloat between feelings of abandonment and loss and his growing ill-health and addictions which eventually killed him in mysterious circumstances at the age of 40.<br />Whether this gloomy life served him as inspiration or he released his pain into his work, the extremeness of his imaginative creations managed to capture attention, if not acceptance.<br /><br /><i>The sickness-the nausea-<br />The pitiless pain-<br />Have ceased, with the fever<br />That maddened my brain-<br />With the fever called "Living"<br />That burned in my brain.</i><br /><br />Considered the father of the short story, Poe manages to control the soul of the reader, nothing intervenes or distracts once you are engulfed in one of his curious and terrifying tales, you feel pulled down by an inexplicable and exotic sort of nostalgia which catches at your breath and prevents you from stopping to read. But make no mistake, Poe plays with you, giving you hope in a futile attempt to search for the truth and offer a plausible explanation for the unaccountable, even though you know deep inside that the end will be doomed from the start.<br />His literary quality is irrefutable, he borrows from the European Gothic tradition and adds elements of detective stories, creating a new register which seeks for the horrendous truth, for the paincuts into your soul, although sometimes a rare kind of beauty oozes from the text, whether conscious or unconsciously I can't say:<br /><br /><i> Then silence, stillness, and night were the universe</i><br /><br />But mainly, Poe appears as a ruthless, crude and pessimistic voice who wants to put order amid the chaos, who wants to explain the inexplicable to elevate the name of the artist; offering an alternative to the newly born optimism, complacency and materialism of his age, and asking for nothing in return. He didn't seek for approval and often had to endure rebuke, few of his contemporaries valued his work at the time and being considered an oddball he was banned from society (or he excluded himself willingly).<br />It is through the anguish and torment expressed in his poems and short stories that it is plausible to imagine his existence rather miserable and that he suffered from a precariously balanced state of mind. But then, once again, I ask myself the same question which always arises when I try to link the real life of a writer with his work, was it his eccentricity that made his works so special? Were they the product of a genius or a deranged mind ? Or both?<br /><br /><i>The truth is, I am heartily sick of this life, and of the nineteenth century in general. I am convinced that everything is going wrong. Besides, I am anxious to know who will be President in 2045. As soon, therefore, as I shave and swallow a cup of coffee, I shall step over to Ponnonner's and get embalmed for a couple of hundred years.</i><br /><br />In any case, although his haunted mind offered no respite, Poe's lucid writing managed to push the scales of reality and redefine the artistic world of beauty and lyricism towards a new daring approach where the probability of terror and darkness prevailed and where the <i>motto</i> could be summed up as <i>
  <b>to deny what is, and explain what is not </b>
</i>.<br /><br />As it usual happens in real life, neither black nor white, just a blurred smudge of indistinct grey.</span>
  <a data-text-id="858994087343896168" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1467842300" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1467842300" />
    <a title="Kyriakos Sorokkou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5927877-kyriakos-sorokkou"><img alt="Kyriakos Sorokkou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1585580294p2/5927877.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1467842300?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kyriakos Sorokkou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kyriakos Sorokkou" href="/user/show/5927877-kyriakos-sorokkou">Kyriakos Sorokkou</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781904633136" href="/book/show/515826.Tales_of_Mystery_Imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1467842300" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13667619924924335952"><b>Tales of Mystery & Imagination</b><br/>Since I've written mini reviews for each of the stories I will have them here slightly edited along with an overall rating and review for the collection as a whole.<br/><br/><b>1) The Gold Bug</b><br/>A treasure hunt story involving cryptography, directions, clues, skulls, and a lot of digging. It felt like watching Dora the Little Explorer but in a much darker atmosphere. 4 main characters, the narrator, his friend Mr Legrand, Legrand's dog and Legrand's servant, a nigger, yes another s</span>
  <span id="freeText13667619924924335952" style="display:none"><b>Tales of Mystery &amp; Imagination</b><br />Since I've written mini reviews for each of the stories I will have them here slightly edited along with an overall rating and review for the collection as a whole.<br /><br /><b>1) The Gold Bug</b><br />A treasure hunt story involving cryptography, directions, clues, skulls, and a lot of digging. It felt like watching Dora the Little Explorer but in a much darker atmosphere. 4 main characters, the narrator, his friend Mr Legrand, Legrand's dog and Legrand's servant, a nigger, yes another story that has racist remarks. Anyway this was a 3.5 stars story nothing more than a treasure hunt. But I've enjoyed it more than others. <br /><br /><b>2) The Facts in the Case of M. Valdemar</b><br />A practitioner of mesmerism (a precursor of hypnosis) tries through the medium of hypnosis/mesmerism to revive a recently deceased person. A macabre little tale worthy of 3 stars. Didn't blew my mind though.<br /><br /><b>3) MS. Found in a Bottle</b><br />A story about a man that scrambles aboard a gigantic ship manned by elderly crewmen who are unable to see him (possibly a ghost ship). A sea tale that ends abruptly and with a sense of doom. 3 stars because I needed more, something that Mr. Poe rarely does. <br /><br /><b>4) A Descent into the Mælström</b><br />At the summit of a mountain climb in Lofoten (mentioned in Kavvadias Ο Πιλότος Νάγκελ), in Norway, an old Norwegian is telling the story of his near escape from a massive whirlpool called Mælström. Interesting story but there's no actual rising action or suspense because you know that the narrator is going to survive since he's the one telling the story. Almost 3 stars.<br /><br /><b>5) The Murders in the Rue Morgue</b><br />A tale that is considered the first detective story, written 46 years before the first Sherlock Holmes story in 1887. A gruesome double murder is committed in Rue Morgue in Paris and amateur detective C. Auguste Dupin investigates the crime scene. What he finds is beyond suspicion. 3.5<br /><br /><b>6) The Mystery of Marie Rogêt</b><br />A sequel to the previous story. The longest and most boring story so far. It doesn't have a plot. Dupin just discusses with the narrator whether the murdered girl was murdered and <i>then</i> thrown into the river or the other way round, whether her clothes were torn intentionally or not, or whether the newspaper articles were telling the truth or were inventing things about the murder &amp;c. You will enjoy this essay-like story only if you are a forensics/criminology student. 2 stars<br /><br /><b>7) The Purloined Letter</b><br /> Definitely better story than <i>The Mystery of Marie Rogêt</i> but definitely not as good as <i>The Murders in the Rue Morgue</i>, or moreover an enjoyable reading. It is a discussion more or less of how Dupin managed to take back the Purloined Letter from the villain of the story (a blackmailer) and give it back to the police. They also talk about versions of reality and mathematics. 2.9 stars<br /><br /><b>8) The Fall of the House of Usher</b><br />Finally, a decent gothic story by Mr. Poe in this collection. Our narrator arrives at the gloomy, gothic, decaying house of his childhood friend Roderick Usher and the atmosphere is oppressive even for the reader who anticipates this fall of the house that looms over the story like a threat. An eerie story that you enjoy even though some elements remain inexplicable. 3,5 stars<br /><br /><b>9) The Pit and the Pendulum</b><br />From Wikipedia: <i>Terror is the feeling of dread and anticipation that precedes the horrifying experience. By contrast, horror is the feeling of revulsion that usually occurs after something frightening is seen, heard, or experienced.</i><br />So this is a terror story, the narrator describes his experience of being tortured next to a pit full of rats and a pendulum above him is about to cut him in half. The story was a disappointment though even to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/346732.George_R_R__Martin" title="George R.R. Martin" rel="nofollow noopener">George R.R. Martin</a>, who while at high school he changed the story into a much gruesome and horrible ending which I approve. <br /><br /><b>10) The Premature Burial</b><br />A horror short story on the theme of being buried alive (!)<br />In the first part the narrator describes different cases of premature burials from where some victims escaped and some not!<br />In the second part he describes his own experience as a man who suffers from anxiety of being buried alive. Graves, corpses, tombs, graveyards, mausoleums. Everything I like to read this time of the year. 3.5<br /><br /><b>11) The Black Cat</b><br />Violence against animals, which is followed by a series of ghastly revenges. The narrator, a most unlikable character. He deserved everything he suffered (IMO). Moreover the story is a critique of the perverse actions brought on by alcoholism. 3.5<br /><br /><b>12) The Masque of the Red Death</b><br />The title says it all: Death. The story starts and ends with death. <br />All the characters of the story die drenched in blood. 3.5<br /><br /><b>13) The Cask of Amontillado</b><br />This was one of the first stories I've ever read in English back in 2009 when my English was worse than Tsipras's. Since then I've read it at least 3 more times. A favourite story of revenge, wine, and murder. . . 4 stars.<br /><br /><b>14) The Oval Portrait</b><br />From Wikipedia: <i>"The Oval Portrait" is a short story [...] involving the disturbing circumstances surrounding a portrait in a chateau. It is one of his shortest stories, filling only two pages in its initial publication in 1842.</i><br />I believe this tale might have inspired <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3565.Oscar_Wilde" title="Oscar Wilde" rel="nofollow noopener">Oscar Wilde</a> to write his only novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6411433.The_Picture_of_Dorian_Gray" title="The Picture of Dorian Gray by Oscar Wilde" rel="nofollow noopener">The Picture of Dorian Gray</a>. 3.5<br /><br /><b>15) The Oblong Box</b><br />Nice little story but of course I saw what was coming. A mysterious box belonging to an even more mysterious man, on a ship full of passengers. 3 stars because it was simply interesting but not long enough.<br /><br /><b>16) The Tell-Tale Heart</b><br />A story that was pretty similar with the <i>Black Cat</i> but with a more sinister feeling and a very unreliable insane person as a narrator. The ending was the same with Black Cat's ending so I was a bit disappointed to read the same thing again. 3.5 stars though.<br /><br /><b>17) Ligeia</b><br />The story can be divided into 3 parts.<br />Part 1 where the unnamed narrator describes his wife Ligeia, her appearance and her mind and then she falls ill and dies.<br />Part 2 where the narrator moves to an unnamed gothic abbey in England marries a second wife Rowena who also falls ill and dies.<br />Part 3 is where the supernatural elements of the story come alive. . . Can't say more, you have to read it yourself. Atmospheric and eerie, yet verbose and slow. 3 stars<br /><br /><b>18) Loss of Breath</b><br />What a weird little story. A man literally loses his breath and everybody thinks he's a corpse and so they throw him out of a carriage, they dissect him, they hang him, they bury him, but he can't feel anything because he's out of breath! It was strange and macabre but the concept didn't convince me. How is it possible to live after losing your breath? Well it's fiction but again. . . 3 stars<br /><br /><b>19) Shadow - A Parable</b><br />A story 3 pages long and it left me standing in the shadows. After 2 readings I was still feeling like an ignoramus. 2 stars<br /><br /><b>20) Silence - A Fable</b><br />A fable about a demon and a man in an enchanted land. The demon tells his story, the man listens, and I am confused. Again. . . <br />Can't say more about this story because I simply can't. I just need to point out that the epigraph of this story was in Ancient Greek. <br /><i>εὕδουσι δ΄ ὀρέων κορυφαί τε καὶ φάραγγες πρώονές τε καὶ χαράδραι</i><br />Since the feeling of the story was quite eerie I will give it 2.9 stars. I'm a good man. <br /><br /><b>21) The Man of the Crowd</b><br />A man follows an old man through a crowded London for almost two days and then he decides to stop following him because the old man <i>is the man of the crowd. [...] worse than the Hortulus Anime; and perhaps it is one of the great mercies of God that er laßt sich nicht lesen.</i><br />Τι λες σοβαρά; Σουαχίλι γιατί ξέχασες να γράψεις; Again no explanation, which left me disappointed even though following a man for such a long time is quite creepy. 3 stars<br /><br /><b>22) Some Words with a Mummy</b><br />Finally, the last story, and an interesting one. 4 stars.<br />A group of intellectuals try to revive a mummy and the mummy now revived begins a conversation with the men. It was a witty story, a satire on Epyptomania and a criticism on the supposed superiority of the west. The end was one of the best parts of the story.<br /><i>My wife is a shrew. The truth is, I am heartily sick of this life, and of the nineteenth century in general. I am convinced that everything is going wrong. Besides, I am anxious to know who will be president in 2045. As soon, therefore, as I shave and swallow a cup of coffee, I shall just step over to Ponnonner's and get embalmed for a couple of hundred years.</i><br />To be honest I'm anxious too to know who will be president of the United States in 2045; <b>if</b> they survive the (likely) Armageddon called Donald Trump. <br /><br /><br /><b>Overall</b><br />70.2/22=3.19 which translates into 3 stars. A satisfying collection I had lying unread on my shelves since 2011 but not something I enjoyed 100% Certainly I will buy the 'sequel' to this collection <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2077759.Tales_and_Poems" title="Tales and Poems by Edgar Allan Poe" rel="nofollow noopener">Tales and Poems</a> but I don't think I will bother buying all his writings since it's obvious I will be disappointed by most of them. <br />Well done if you've reached this gargantuan, lengthy, sheety review. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13667619924924335952" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_486719152" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/486719152" />
    <a title="Kyle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11665678-kyle"><img alt="Kyle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1362814696p2/11665678.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/486719152?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kyle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kyle" href="/user/show/11665678-kyle">Kyle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9781841939469" href="/book/show/6668656-tales-of-mystery-imagination">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Crazy people who think they aren't crazy</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer486719152" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1131452682887376374">Poe is now a household name. An American institution. Everyone knows Poe, though few have really delved into more than one or two of his short stories. I'm also pretty sure he was completely insane. Which means he was brilliant, and everyone should read his work.<br/><br/>As the title of the book says, there are many different stories in here, and admittedly, they are not all of the same caliber. It seems that Poe generally became a better writer as he wrote more and more stuff, and I think his later work</span>
  <span id="freeText1131452682887376374" style="display:none">Poe is now a household name. An American institution. Everyone knows Poe, though few have really delved into more than one or two of his short stories. I'm also pretty sure he was completely insane. Which means he was brilliant, and everyone should read his work.<br /><br />As the title of the book says, there are many different stories in here, and admittedly, they are not all of the same caliber. It seems that Poe generally became a better writer as he wrote more and more stuff, and I think his later work is (in general!) superior to his earlier work. Some stories in this compendium are 3-star stories, and some are 5-star stories, with the remainder taking 4-stars. All of Poe's popular and well-known shorts are in here, <i> The Cask of Amontillado, The Tell-Tale Heart, The Fall of The House of Usher</i> all make their respective appearances. Some of my favorites ones however, were his lesser known works <i>William Wilson, Morella, and A Descent Into the Malestrom</i> were all fantastic surprises to me. There was even a delightful comedy stowed away in here in the form of <i>Some Passages in the Life of A Lion (Lionizing)</i>. Imagine that; an actual comedy from Poe!<br /><br />Though Poe chose the short story as his main body of work, there is a common theme about Poe's work. Poe is, ultimately, a blender. A boundary weakener. A line eraser. The boundaries between life and death, good and evil, one world and another. The line between soundness and insanity. None of these are sacred to Edgar Allan Poe's tinkering, and you can't help but admire Poe for the artful way he manipulates the reader's preconceptions.<br />His methods inspired hosts of other writers to explore the same themes (H.P. Lovecraft obviously, and some of Robert Louis Stevenson's work can all be traced to Poe's influence), yet Poe remains an independent, unique, and terrifyingly brilliant voice.<br /><br />In short: Read Poe! You might hate him and go insane (ah well, can't get them all), you might love him and still go crazy (face it, you were probably crazy to begin with), OR you could become entranced by Poe's stories and start a "Poe Boy's" fan club. Whichever way, you should never be forgiven for not reading as much Poe as you can. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1131452682887376374" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18117889918496234613">I have to open with a confession, for those of you who do not know I am a self-confessed Poe obsessive so there is every possibility that this review is a tad biased.   <span id="freeText18117889918496234613" style="display:none">I have to open with a confession, for those of you who do not know I am a self-confessed Poe obsessive so there is every possibility that this review is a tad biased. Having said that this collection of 10 tales and poems shows his genius at capturing the darker side of humanity, from the ability to torment and torture to the sometimes unbelievable determination to survive, even when pounding on death's door. This collection includes some of my absolute favourites from Poe including The Raven, which for me is one of the darkest and most heartbreaking poems ever, and The Tell-Tale Heart, which is more than disturbing with it's glimpse into the mind of the guilty. Each tale/poem is illustrated by a different artist so the styles vary with each but they all manage to capture the essence of the associated story and I'm sure Edgar would be rather happy with them too. An excellent collection and a good introduction to one of the masters of the macabre should you need it.<br /><br />The full contents is:<br />MS. Found in a Bottle<br />The Raven<br />Hop-Frog<br />The Tell-Tale Heart<br />The Black Cat<br />The Conqueror Worm<br />The Oval Portrait<br />The Bells<br />The Pit and the Pendulum<br />The Thousand-and-Second Tale of Scheherazade<br />The Masque of the Red Death<br />The Facts in the Case of M. Valdemar<br />A Descent into the Maelstrom</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1081661217" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11498233609439496209"><b>Tales of Mystery and Imagination</b> is a great compilation of short stories by Egdar Allan Poe, including some of his most appraised works, like "The Tell-Tale Heart", "The Black Cat", and "The Murders in the Rue Morgue"; and quite a few of lesser known - and also great - works, such as "The Colloquy of Monos and Una" and "Berenice". This collection was a good mixture of horror and mystery, and I highly recommend checking out Poe's works if you haven't already!</span>

<div id="review_3015599855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Dimitrije Srebric" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/51268667-dimitrije-srebric"><img alt="Dimitrije Srebric" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1602149407p2/51268667.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3015599855?book_show_action=true">Mar 22, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dimitrije Srebric" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dimitrije Srebric" href="/user/show/51268667-dimitrije-srebric">Dimitrije Srebric</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/28595402-tales-of-mystery-and-imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51268667-dimitrije-srebric?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/51268667-dimitrije-srebric?shelf=horror">horror</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3015599855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16961536947823280578">Some stories in this collection are better than others. For example, Edgar Allan Poe's detective stories such as "The Murders in Rue Morgue" or "The Mystery of Marie Rogêt" were inferior in quality in comparison with "The Fall of the House of Usher", "The Pit and the Pendulum", and my absolute favorite, "The Black Cat". This story is, to my mind, the pinnacle of Poe's macabre genius (as far as his prose goes), as it shows vividly the gradual mental breakdown from first person perspective. Not to</span>
  <span id="freeText16961536947823280578" style="display:none">Some stories in this collection are better than others. For example, Edgar Allan Poe's detective stories such as "The Murders in Rue Morgue" or "The Mystery of Marie Rogêt" were inferior in quality in comparison with "The Fall of the House of Usher", "The Pit and the Pendulum", and my absolute favorite, "The Black Cat". This story is, to my mind, the pinnacle of Poe's macabre genius (as far as his prose goes), as it shows vividly the gradual mental breakdown from first person perspective. Not to mention it really pushed me to the limit in regard to my disgust of animal torture! The idea of a slow, but impending doom is also the theme of his second best story "The Pit and the Pendulum". <br /><br />As said previously, Poe is a genius, and a master of macabre, but his talent is even better expressed in his poetry, which this book sadly lacks, so I had to acquire his poetry collection separately.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16961536947823280578" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <span id="freeText17689654113517530514" style="display:none">First who reads Poe to their kiddies though I suppose it's no worse than some of the fairy stories the kids like. Speaking of fairy tales, though I know it's not his best, I couldn't help wishing 'The Island of the Fay' was included not because it's his best story but because I think it would make for an evocative illustration. Obviously not all of Poe's stories are included in this volume though probably two-thirds are. I think every story has a picture though not all have a color illustration. Some of the black and and white pictures are as good as the color ones. Oddly the color illustrations aren't with the story they depict but scattered throughout the book. None have more than four or five colors and I believe I counted ten color illustrations in all. The black and white pictures are with thei proper text and two that stood out are of 'The Pit and the Pendulum' and 'The Muders in the Rue Morgue'. The Pit pic is notable for the horrified expression and the Rue Morgue because the shadowed figures trail lacelike tendrils from he ladies gowns and some of th gents clothes and hair and even the dogs fur which helps depict their made race to find the killer. The pictures are evocative of the 19th century though per the back of the title pages it states that Arthur Rackham's Poe work was first shown in 1935.<br /><br />A word about the volume itself; it has no dust jacket to lose in the nursery but it's sturdy and has a small set in color illustration on the cover. There's also a slate gray silk cord to mark your place in the book.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_2665915432" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2665915432" />
    <a title="Diana Iozzia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66291103-diana-iozzia"><img alt="Diana Iozzia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503597610p2/66291103.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2665915432?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Diana Iozzia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Diana Iozzia" href="/user/show/66291103-diana-iozzia">Diana Iozzia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2665915432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15443382021869393180">"Tales of Mystery and Imagination"<br/><br/>Written by Edgar Allan Poe<br/><br/>Review written by Diana Iozzia<br/><br/>The collection of short stories by Edgar Allan Poe, known as "Tales of Mystery and Imagination" is a wonderful classic in the horror and mystery genres. I have been a reader of Edgar Allan Poe for years, for at least most of my childhood and adulthood. Due to this, I had read a few of the stories in the collection, but I was relatively impressed by the other ones I had not yet heard of.<br/><br/>"The Gold Bug" is a m</span>
  <span id="freeText15443382021869393180" style="display:none">"Tales of Mystery and Imagination"<br /><br />Written by Edgar Allan Poe<br /><br />Review written by Diana Iozzia<br /><br />The collection of short stories by Edgar Allan Poe, known as "Tales of Mystery and Imagination" is a wonderful classic in the horror and mystery genres. I have been a reader of Edgar Allan Poe for years, for at least most of my childhood and adulthood. Due to this, I had read a few of the stories in the collection, but I was relatively impressed by the other ones I had not yet heard of.<br /><br />"The Gold Bug" is a mystery including puzzles and codes. I personally enjoyed this, because it reminded by of "The Most Dangerous Game". We start to suspect that the narrator's friend has a threatening motivation behind the hunt for treasure on which they have embarked.<br /><br />"The Facts in the Case of M. Valdemar" is a strange sort of "campfire" story or story that you'd tell at a sleepover. It is an archetypal ghost story. Our narrator is interested in mesmerism and hypnotism. He tells of his friend who was in a comatose state, but kept alive in a mesmerized state.<br /><br />"Message Found in a Bottle" has a narrator who writes in his diary as a whirlpool consumes his crew and ship.<br /><br />"A Descent into the Maelstrom" features a narrator who is told a story of a man delving into a maelstrom. I personally was not charmed by this one. <br /><br />"The Murders in the Rue Morgue", "The Mystery of Marie Roget", and "The Purloined Letter" were the biggest disappointments for me in this collection. I was astounded by my dislike for Poe's mystery short stories. They were unrealistic and boring to me. <br /><br />"The Fall of the House of Usher" is one of my favorite films starring Vincent Price. I was surprised by the lack of detail and plot in the actual story it was based on. I still liked the plot: a man visits his old friend, whose own mental health and that of his sister's has been ruined by the house.<br /><br />"The Pit and the Pendulum" features a character plagued by imprisonment in a horrific pit.<br /><br />"The Premature Burial" features smaller vignettes about people who are at the brink of death, but are buried alive. <br /><br />"The Black Cat" was one of my favorite stories that I found through this collection. I had heard of it before, but had not read it. It features a man spiral through different levels of insanity, haunted by his murdered cat.<br /><br />"The Cask of the Amontillado" and "The Tell Tale Heart" are stories I know like the back of my hand and love. <br /><br />"The Oblong Box" features a man who encounters strange occurrences while on a cruise with his wife. I thoroughly enjoyed this one.<br /><br />"Ligeia" hosts a narrator who talks about the odd mysteries surrounding his wife's death.<br /><br />"Silence: A Fable" has a demon recall his journey through a dark abyss.<br /><br />"The Man of the Crowd" features a narrator follow and observe a mysterious man in London.<br /><br />"Some Words with a Mummy" is a more comedic story. A group of scientists meet a man who has been mummified that rises and speaks to them. <br /><br />All in all, I enjoyed reading this collection. I personally love short stories, especially the works of Edgar Allan Poe. I have to say, I thought the glossary and index in this book was not at all helpful for understanding the stories.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15443382021869393180" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2585120201" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2585120201" />
    <a title="carlageek" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17679151-carlageek"><img alt="carlageek" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1361456132p2/17679151.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2585120201?book_show_action=true">Feb 15, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="carlageek" class="user" itemprop="url" name="carlageek" href="/user/show/17679151-carlageek">carlageek</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/17679151-carlageek?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2585120201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1604907111557207010">I came to understand, while reading this collection, that Poe is remembered for his ideas, rather than his writing. He is often credited as the origin of such innovations as the unreliable narrator and the modern detective story, and such inventiveness is amply on display in these stories. But it’s blended with object lessons for any beginning writer struggling with such technical matters as the importance of selecting the right point of view for a story, or the value of showing over telling. <br/><br/>In</span>
  <span id="freeText1604907111557207010" style="display:none">I came to understand, while reading this collection, that Poe is remembered for his ideas, rather than his writing. He is often credited as the origin of such innovations as the unreliable narrator and the modern detective story, and such inventiveness is amply on display in these stories. But it’s blended with object lessons for any beginning writer struggling with such technical matters as the importance of selecting the right point of view for a story, or the value of showing over telling. <br /><br />In short, Poe’s ability to invent a story is magnificent. His ability to tell one, however, is middling. Too often he provides a first-person narrator at a remove from the story, so that the narrator is being told a summary of the actual story by another character who is more central to it. The detective stories featuring C. Auguste Dupin, for instance, are related by a first-person narrator to whom Dupin tells the actual story, in summary masquerading as dialogue. (Other 19th-century writers suffered from this same contorted POV problem; Mary W Shelley’s <i>Frankenstein</i> and Emily Bronte’s <i>Wuthering Heights</i> leap to mind.) Or a narrative that begins inside the story will then take a leap away from it, as does “The Gold Bug,” which begins with a first-person narrator trying to make sense out of his friend's apparent madness, appears to climax with the friend’s vindication as they dig up a pirate’s massive treasure, and then disintegrates with a coda longer than the story itself in which the friend explains to the narrator how he came to discover the treasure map, how he deciphered the encoded message it contained, and so on. <br /><br />The trouble with this kind of narrative distance is that it sucks the energy out a story. There is no suspense at all in the decipherment of the treasure map, because the reader has already seen the treasure discovered. There’s likewise diminished suspense in “Descent into the Maelstrom,” for the man who experienced the harrowing ride on a ship sucked down into a huge ocean vortex is sitting on a rock, relating that experience to the story’s narrator. And it’s a terrible waste of some of Poe’s excellent ideas. <br /><br />All that technical complaining aside, there are some superb stories in this volume, and the ones that are best known are generally also the best executed. “The Cask of Amontillado” is everything for which one reads Poe, with its demented unreliable narrator, rationalizing a particularly cruel murder while never telling the reader exactly what wrong was done to him that justifies it in his mind. In “The Tell-Tale Heart,” a murderer’s conscience famously gets the better of him. And “William Wilson,” my overall favorite, shows a dissolute man with a splintered identity, at war with his own conscience, which he perceives as a separately incarnated man. I read that Patricia Highsmith was particularly influenced by Poe, and I believe it; one can see quite distinctly in these stories the embryos of men like Charlie Bruno, Guy Haines, and Tom Ripley.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1604907111557207010" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3084192483" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3084192483" />
    <a title="John Mccullough" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13417829-john-mccullough"><img alt="John Mccullough" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349487620p2/13417829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3084192483?book_show_action=true">Jan 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Mccullough" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Mccullough" href="/user/show/13417829-john-mccullough">John Mccullough</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007420223" href="/book/show/12410969-tales-of-mystery-and-imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13417829-john-mccullough?shelf=american-literature">american-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13417829-john-mccullough?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13417829-john-mccullough?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13417829-john-mccullough?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3084192483" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2155570286303151662">Poe is the pioneer of American literature in several genres. Detective fiction, Gothic horror, perhaps even science fiction? Included in this collection are some of Poe’s best stories, including “The Cask of Amontillado,” “The Fall of the House of Usher,” The Gold Bug,” “The Mystery of Marie Rogêt,” “The Purloined Letter,” and “The Murders in the Rue Morgue.” I enjoyed them all. As a freshman at Penn State, Poe’s stories were required reading the second semester and I enjoyed them just as much t</span>
  <span id="freeText2155570286303151662" style="display:none">Poe is the pioneer of American literature in several genres. Detective fiction, Gothic horror, perhaps even science fiction? Included in this collection are some of Poe’s best stories, including “The Cask of Amontillado,” “The Fall of the House of Usher,” The Gold Bug,” “The Mystery of Marie Rogêt,” “The Purloined Letter,” and “The Murders in the Rue Morgue.” I enjoyed them all. As a freshman at Penn State, Poe’s stories were required reading the second semester and I enjoyed them just as much then as now.<br /><br />The Mystery of Marie Rogêt is a fictionalized version of an actual murder that occurred in the New York City area, but Poe sets the scene in the Paris of Inspector Dupin. It is a critique of contemporary newspaper accounts of Mary Cecelia Rogers’ disappearance and the possible discovery of her body floating in the river. As such it is also a discourse on early 19th century forensic analysis of the materials appearing in the newspapers. It is thought to be “the first murder mystery based on the details of a real crime” according to the Wikipedia article. I will guess it is also of little interest to any but those fascinated by murder or the details of forensic analysis.<br /><br />“The Murders in the Rue Morgue” is the first “detective” story by Poe and the one most often read and mentioned. C. Auguste Dupin is introduced in this story, the first of three appearances; given his deductive methods, Dupin has to be an early version of Britain’s own Sherlock Holmes. Aside the surprise ending, could this be the first, or an early, version of a “locked room mystery?”<br /><br />As a biological anthropologist I was also interested in several other stories for strictly professional reasons. “The Fall of the House of Usher” seems to feature a family cursed either by tuberculosis or, because of the intergenerational description, perhaps a genetic disorder such as phenylketonuria (PKU), although in the long run, it is just a curse meant to horrify a gothic-loving audience.<br /><br />Last, Poe’s contribution to literary humor – or sarcasm – is seen in such stories as “The Devil in the Belfry,” and an early version of “1066 and All That,” in his interpretation of Scheherazade’s very last story. The horrible “Masque of the Red Death” might have been written after reading Boccaccio’s “The Decameron.”<br /><br />So much has been written about Poe that I will not duplicate the already abundant literature. I will note that Poe richly describes the settings and the characters so that you feel to be a part of the scene and almost interacting with the characters. Thus, he is also a master of the gothic novel very early on. The book is a classic and a grand compendium of Poe’s best stories although some elements are certainly dated and an annotated version would occasionally help with some minutae. Be sure to have this on your MUST READ list soon!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2155570286303151662" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_119508412" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119508412" />
    <a title="Brad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1188312-brad"><img alt="Brad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1296434017p2/1188312.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119508412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14313096867102328758">We did it! Jess and I have been reading this one out loud together for over three years, nearly the whole time that we have been in Ohio. We weren't terribly consistent over the latter part of that time, but we made a big push in the past week to get through the last hundred pages or so. <br/><br/>Overall a pretty good anthology that showcases most of Poe's well-known works as well as many that I had never heard of before. While I wouldn't recommend it for everyone, it is a classic and scratches a gothic </span>
  <span id="freeText14313096867102328758" style="display:none">We did it! Jess and I have been reading this one out loud together for over three years, nearly the whole time that we have been in Ohio. We weren't terribly consistent over the latter part of that time, but we made a big push in the past week to get through the last hundred pages or so. <br /><br />Overall a pretty good anthology that showcases most of Poe's well-known works as well as many that I had never heard of before. While I wouldn't recommend it for everyone, it is a classic and scratches a gothic itch. Average of 2.886 stars over all of the stories.<br /><br />Rating: PG-13, because of murder.<br /><br />THE GOLD BUG (4/5)<br />I had expectations for this one to be more of a horror story than an adventure, but it was a fine tale nonetheless. I was surprised that Poe described all the steps (accurately) of simple substitution cipher decryption. Pretty great story.<br /><br />THE FACTS IN THE CASE OF M. VALDEMAR (4/5)<br />This story was kind of creepy, and was more of what I had expected from this book. Poe ventures a little into the unknown, and ends with a somewhat grotesque finale. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The man somehow suspends his life and lives beyond death. When he ends his experiment, I think he ages and decays very rapidly, Indiana Jones style. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />MS FOUND IN A BOTTLE (2/5)<br />Meh, not really that exciting. Maybe because I'm not one who's terrible intrigued by the sea? Granted, it made me curious about the old men on the ship who spake in a different tongue and seemed to be on a voyage of discovery (snicker).<br /><br />A DESCENT INTO THE MAELSTROM (4/5)<br />Ok, this was a good sea story. The narrator looks down on a massive whirlpool with a guide who survived it. The story is all about the whirlpool and how the guide made it through. Exciting.<br /><br />THE MURDERS IN THE RUE MORGUE (4/5)<br />This story reminded me of Sherlock Holmes. Dupin has a similar mind, and is able to figure things out from clues that most people would have overlooked. In this story, he figures out who committed a horrible murder and how it was done. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[The murder was committed by an orangutan who had swung his way in to the house. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE MYSTERY OF MARIE ROGET (2/5)<br />Another story of Dupin, and thus reminiscent of Holmes. However, this story seemed to drag on and on, and seemed heavy on details that we didn't care about and light on the actual resolution of the story.<br /><br />THE PURLOINED LETTER (3/5)<br />Dupin returns again, and figures out where a scoundrel has hidden a blackmail letter that he stole. The police thoroughly search the scoundrel's apartment without a trace of the letter, but <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Dupin figures it must be hidden in plain sight; he goes to the apartment, finds the letter, leaves his snuffbox, returns home to make a fake letter, returns the next day for his snuffbox and switches the letters. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE FALL OF THE HOUSE OF USHER (2/5)<br />This is one of the stories that I had heard of previously, but had no idea what it was about. As far as I could tell, it's about <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[an estate that is somehow linked to its owners, the Usher family. The last remaining members of the family, a brother and sister, are in a depressive funk, and then the sister is presumed to die. She is placed in a coffin in the keep of the estate, but it turns out she's not really dead--she comes to her brother, bloody from working herself out of her coffin, and she falls on him, dead. The shock (?) kills the brother, the narrator leaves the house, which then cracks down the middle. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE PIT AND THE PENDULUM (3/5)<br />Another story I was somewhat familiar with from before. Probably would have received an additional star if there wasn't so much dilly-dallying in the beginning. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Our narrator is a victim of the Spanish Inquisition, and this story details his torture--almost falling in the pit, barely escaping from the sharp pendulum with the help of some rats, and then almost falling into the pit as the walls are heated and closing in. Luckily he is rescued at the last minute. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE PREMATURE BURIAL (2/5)<br />Kind of a boring story about several people who were thought to be dead, but weren't. The narrator then finds himself in a similar situation after his years of obsession and cataplexy, except that <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he's actually just waking up in the tight berth of a ship he was on <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>.<br /><br />THE BLACK CAT (4/5)<br />Ah, this was excellent--short and sweet. No lengthy monologues by the narrator, just a good telling of a spooky story. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[A guy who loves animals as a kid becomes a drunk in later life. He starts mistreating his pets and wife, and when his cat bites him he gouges out one of its eyes. Eventually in his frustration he also hangs the cat, and that night his house burns down. He later finds another, similar cat that follows him home, but eventually frustrates him. He swings an axe at it, but his wife stops him. He then kills his wife with the axe and hides her in the wall. He walls the cat up with her, and the cat yowls when the police investigate the home. The corpse is discovered and the man goes to the gallows. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE MASQUE OF THE RED DEATH (3/5)<br />Interesting imagery, and I liked the infectious "bad guy." The colored rooms seemed like an interesting place for a party.<br /><br />THE CASK OF AMONTILLADO (4/5)<br />A creepy revenge story. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I liked the way that the narrator "tried" to disuade Fortunato from continuing along, and that only strengthened Fortunato's resolve. And then he got walled into the crypt. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE OVAL PAINTING (2/5)<br />A very lifelike painting is discovered by the narrator. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[He reads about it and finds out that it was of the artist's wife, and that he was so focused on making the painting come to life that his wife died as he finished it. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE OBLONG BOX (2/5)<br />The twist in this story probably was awesome back when Poe wrote the story, but is pretty easy to guess now. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[A man brings his dead wife's body on a trans-Atlantic voyage. The wife's maid impersonates the wife for the trip without anyone being the wiser. The man's friends discovers what was in the oblong box (the body) after the man tied himself to it and threw himself into the sea after a terrible storm. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />THE TELL-TALE HEART (4/5)<br />Excellent story, and rightfully one of Poe's better known tales. Short and sweet.<br /><br />LIGEIA (2.5/5)<br />Reeeaally slow to start, but had a great ending. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[Guy describes his first wife in painstakingly verbose prose. She dies. He remarries a lady who marries him for his money. They don't get along, she gets sick, and then some invisible thing poisons her and she dies. The corpse is reanimated and Ligeia has returned (by force of will?) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />LOSS OF BREATH (4/5)<br />This one caught me off guard because of how humorous it was--pretty funny story of a guy who loses his breath when yelling at his wife. Then, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[he gets sat on in a carriage, is thought to be dead, is taken to a surgeon who cuts off his ears and removes some of his bowel, has his nose eaten off by cats, jumps out the window of a turret into a wagon of a criminal who looks just like him, is hung, and is buried. In the sepulcher he soliloquizes about the other dead around him. One of them happens to be the neighbor his wife fancied, who coincidentally has too much breath. He transfers one to the protagonist, and all ends well. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />SHADOW - A PARABLE (1/5)<br />Um, I guess I didn't get it. Short. Maybe the point being that death comes for everyone? Dunno.<br /><br />SILENCE - A FABLE (1/5)<br />Same as above. Didn't really get it--a demon tells a man about another man he met who was only mildly cowed by a large storm the demon called up.<br /><br />THE MAN OF THE CROWD (2/5)<br />? I think that the man was a personification of the crowd? But I'm not really sure. He hurried to places with people and didn't seem himself on his own.<br /><br />SOME WORDS WITH A MUMMY (4/5)<br />A very strong ending for this anthology. Some gentlemen resuscitate a mummy and have a talk with him. Turns out he's a one-upper and that everything was better in ancient Egypt than in modern times. Quite amusing, like Loss of Breath.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14313096867102328758" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
