The stories about people buried alive fascinated me to no end when I was a child, but I think my favorite story now is The Black Cat, because it packs so much sheer evil in so few pages. The stories about adven</span>
  <span id="freeText15223757732379410321" style="display:none">The edition I've read is actually a Spanish translation (in a wonderful 80's edition for children that would certainly not be possible today), but what I love most about it are Arthur Rackham's absolutely terrifying illustrations, accompanied by some equally gruesome ones by Harry Clarke. The stories about people buried alive fascinated me to no end when I was a child, but I think my favorite story now is The Black Cat, because it packs so much sheer evil in so few pages. The stories about adventures at sea and the Maelstrom bore me a little. But it's certainly stil a great read. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11230508897408087296">I gave each of the 22 stories a rating upon finishing them and averaged out on 3,3 stars. It feel very right, while there were a few 5 star stories and a lot of really good 4 star stories, there were also some that just didn’t work for me at all. <br/><br/>I loved “The Purloined Letter”, “The Black Cat”, “The Case of M. Valdemar”, “The Tell-Tale Heart” and “The Murders in the Rue Morgue” most, three of which I had previously read. I am definitely not opposed to reading more Poe and looking forward to it.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=my-personal-canon">my-personal-canon</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=adventure">adventure</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=mystery-and-suspense">mystery-and-suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/30785064-harry-doble?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1983771879" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17160782000509321513">An anthology of Edgar Allan Poe's short stories. Obsessed with the macabre, he typically wrote horror but also in a variety of genres such as dark fantasy, adventure, and detective fiction. Three things are characteristic to all of his stories for me. The first is a lofty writing style that is as concise as it is erudite. Poe was an educated man and his stories frequently reference themes in esoteric philosophy. It is in this sense I can see his influence on another one of my favourite writers, </span>
  <span id="freeText17160782000509321513" style="display:none">An anthology of Edgar Allan Poe's short stories. Obsessed with the macabre, he typically wrote horror but also in a variety of genres such as dark fantasy, adventure, and detective fiction. Three things are characteristic to all of his stories for me. The first is a lofty writing style that is as concise as it is erudite. Poe was an educated man and his stories frequently reference themes in esoteric philosophy. It is in this sense I can see his influence on another one of my favourite writers, Jorge Luis Borges. The second is a strong ability to describe his characters' psychology. Poe often writes in first person and describes the intricacies of consciousness and emotion in striking detail. The third is logic and the reasoning process in general. Characters frequently engage in a dialogue with themselves and others as they try to make sense of things they don't know or understand, whether they are piecing together the clues of a murder mystery (the Dupin stories), searching for a buried treasure location (The Gold Bug), or trying to understand the horrible predicament they are in (The Pit and the Pendulum). There is a lot of time spent rationalizing actions as Poe tries to show empathy towards the minds of those who would commit horrible sins. He does it so well that I can see quite clearly why he was such a towering influence over later developments in horror. <br /><br />These are nearly all very good stories, but some of Poe's shortcomings are that he framed some stories around pseudosciences like phrenology (The Imp of the Perverse) and mesmerism (The Facts in the Case of M. Valdemar) which comes across as very dated. Many of his stories that run over similar themes become very samey as well, especially his stories about sick, dying women (Ligeia, Eleonora, Berenice, and Morella). Arthur Rackham's illustrations in this edition also don't do a lot for me. Otherwise, this is an excellent collection.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17160782000509321513" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1360806021" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1360806021" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10296425-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1339995905p2/10296425.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1360806021?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/10296425-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/26079942-tales-of-mystery-and-imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1360806021" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9375797648532665986">It's almost impossible to review this book, because - now - it's impossible to get the feeling of how it would have been when it first came out one and three-quarter centuries ago. By modern standards, it seems tame, long on atmosphere and short on plot, and above all horrendously pedantic. Many of the stories are twice the length they perhaps should be, by today's standards, filled with asides and unnecessary details. In "The Gold-bug", for example, Poe spends pages explaining how to unravel a </span>
  <span id="freeText9375797648532665986" style="display:none">It's almost impossible to review this book, because - now - it's impossible to get the feeling of how it would have been when it first came out one and three-quarter centuries ago. By modern standards, it seems tame, long on atmosphere and short on plot, and above all horrendously pedantic. Many of the stories are twice the length they perhaps should be, by today's standards, filled with asides and unnecessary details. In "The Gold-bug", for example, Poe spends pages explaining how to unravel a cypher, and the introduction to "The Murders in the Rue Morgue' spend several largely unnecessary pages in the nature of intuition and analytical thought. Despite this, the work is thoroughly enjoyable, if read with the above caveats taken into consideration, and many of the stories it contains are justifiably regarded as classics.<br /><br />But this is not the point. Poe was, above all, an innovator. He was the primary force in the creation of the detective story. Without Dupin, there would be no Sherlock Holmes, no Columbo, no Jonathan Creek, nor any of the myriad of analytical detectives before, between, or since. He also virtually invented the short story, developing it along the basic rule that the weight of every word is vital to the whole (ironic given the amount of unnecessary exposition already mentioned). Poe was also, through this careful weighting of text, the creator of fantastic atmosphere. Poe also gives - arguably - one of the finest literary descriptions of paranoid schizophrenia as seen from the sufferer's viewpoint in "The Tell-tale Heart". Stories such as "The Fall of the House of Usher" rely almost solely on Poe's power of description to weave their spell - in stories which, when written, would have been horrific in their lurid details. That they seem more tame today is not the fault of the writer. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9375797648532665986" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/247573369?book_show_action=true">Dec 15, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pam" href="/user/show/3220056-pam">Pam</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780007420223" href="/book/show/12410969-tales-of-mystery-and-imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3220056-pam?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer247573369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17617080368463787395">This book spent three years in my "currently reading" pile, but now I've finished it. Ha!<br/><br/>The short stories in this collection fall in three main categories. 1) The horrific and/or freaky, 2) the puzzles and detective stories, and 3) the stories about losing loved ones, particular of the romantic kind.<br/><br/>That last one would make anyone wonder whether Poe had lost someone -- he did, his wife of 11 years. (She was 13 when he married her, but let's not go there). The stories, to me, border on the obses</span>
  <span id="freeText17617080368463787395" style="display:none">This book spent three years in my "currently reading" pile, but now I've finished it. Ha!<br /><br />The short stories in this collection fall in three main categories. 1) The horrific and/or freaky, 2) the puzzles and detective stories, and 3) the stories about losing loved ones, particular of the romantic kind.<br /><br />That last one would make anyone wonder whether Poe had lost someone -- he did, his wife of 11 years. (She was 13 when he married her, but let's not go there). The stories, to me, border on the obsessive. The themes and emotions are practically the same, with only the names and settings being changed. It got to a point where I stopped feeling a little sorry for Poe and started getting a bit creeped out.<br /><br />The detective stories are not really that good. People may argue that Poe wrote the first detective story (<i>The Murders in the Rue Morgue</i>), but Conan Doyle still did better with even the least interesting Sherlock Holmes cases. <i>The Gold Bug</i>, which is more a puzzle-type story, is the only story I really liked in this category.<br /><br />Most of my favorites are in the first category: <i>The Fall of the House of Usher</i>, <i>The Pit and the Pendulum</i>, <i>The Masque of the Red Death</i>, and a few others I can't recall right at the moment.<br /><br />And then there were two other stories that don't fall in any of the above categories: <i>The Domain of Arnheim</i> and <i>Landor's Cottage</i>. They were frankly very dull and I'd decided to give up trying to finish the second one.<br /><br />Oh, and Poe writes really well. It's the kind of writing that should be read aloud because of all those beautiful words and turns of phrase. All of the famous writers in his era write beautifully -- they aren't famous for nothing -- but that still deserves to be said.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17617080368463787395" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1313587418" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1313587418" />
    <a title="Stuart" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18203243-stuart"><img alt="Stuart" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364353757p2/18203243.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1313587418?book_show_action=true">Jun 21, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stuart" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stuart" href="/user/show/18203243-stuart">Stuart</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781853260131" href="/book/show/263244.Tales_of_Mystery_and_Imagination">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18203243-stuart?shelf=horror">horror</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1313587418" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7793396740613683710">Lugubrious. I always wanted to use that word, and here, finally, I have a fitting place for it. There is no doubt that literature owes a great debt to Poe. An innovator of and/or inventor of many elements of American gothic suspense; precursor to the detective fiction of Arthur Conan Doyle and simply a good weaver of tales. However, taken collectively in a giant volume, it was too much downward spiral for me. His themes of sadness, revenge, madness, and sardonic bitterness wear one down. Also, h</span>
  <span id="freeText7793396740613683710" style="display:none">Lugubrious. I always wanted to use that word, and here, finally, I have a fitting place for it. There is no doubt that literature owes a great debt to Poe. An innovator of and/or inventor of many elements of American gothic suspense; precursor to the detective fiction of Arthur Conan Doyle and simply a good weaver of tales. However, taken collectively in a giant volume, it was too much downward spiral for me. His themes of sadness, revenge, madness, and sardonic bitterness wear one down. Also, his style is strange. Almost to a story it is worth skipping the first 20%. His build ups are slow and wordy and labor on and on. Then, like a switch, the plots turn on and read well, gripping until the very end. <br /><br />The Premature Burial, Ligeia, Pit and the Pendulum, The Oval Portrait, Imp of the Perverse, Tell Tale Heart. All were fast paced and good reads. <br /><br />The true disappointments for me were the detective novellas: Murder in the Rue Morgue, Marie Roget and the Purloined Letter. His detective, Dupin, is arrogant and smug and prattles on endlessly regarding the mathematics of his reasoning ability. Yet most of it seems to be flash and show by emphasizing the stupidity of others to mislead us in a false direction before surprising us with his genius. Marie Roget was so bad that I simply skipped a small section of it and read the end to find out the solution. <br /><br />I learned a lot about writing style from the collection, enjoying it in small doses, but recommend reading his stories individually over time rather than collectively. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7793396740613683710" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1754147700" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6132144426682109795">Another volume for my recently started Poe collection, this volume has 6 of Poe's better known stories complete with a small number of illustrations to bring hos work to life (as if it needs the help, still they are really good illustrations, the one for Usher is excellent). As ever I am a self confessed Poe addict so this review is biased to the hilt (you have been warned) but no-one can deny he had a knack and talent for bringing his stories to life and capturing the very essence of each of hi</span>
  <span id="freeText6132144426682109795" style="display:none">Another volume for my recently started Poe collection, this volume has 6 of Poe's better known stories complete with a small number of illustrations to bring hos work to life (as if it needs the help, still they are really good illustrations, the one for Usher is excellent). As ever I am a self confessed Poe addict so this review is biased to the hilt (you have been warned) but no-one can deny he had a knack and talent for bringing his stories to life and capturing the very essence of each of his characters and the core of the story. This volume includes The Gold-Bug, a great treasure hunt story that puts you in the mind of Treasure Isand, The Oblong Box, which is a tale of love, loss and superstition, The Mystery of Marie Roget, which takes a real life crime and proposes a solution. The volume also includes The Fall of the House of Usher, one of the first Poe stories I read and one that chills me even today, The Murders in the Rue Morgue, the prequal to Marie Roget and a great murder mystery in its own right and is finished of by The Pit and the Pendulum, which provides a terrifying look into the practices of the Inquisition. If you never read any Poe before (what is wrong with you!?) this showcases his most popular stories and makes a great start. If you have read Poe before, then you'll love it (unless you're not a fan, in which case...what is wrong with you!?!).</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('y7L3tiiJjAQ+5cqkO4BRa2xcpyRbXX8CepSIm/pX8diA82KVcgk2jwEujxx1GV+nkeOAlSPixppGRwCE8AtXxA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fz3sdrXNukXYpNkGZ1F1oNN6XW7hc389U1UZ7IUKtCI0fHlV700AzudvnL4pyHtsLsV635nMxqVvhpHzj1YSPg==')})
  }
//]]>

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">About Edgar Allan Poe</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1454522972p3/4624490.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/4624490.Edgar_Allan_Poe">Edgar Allan Poe
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
20,394
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5237191281102806778">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of</span>
  <span id="freeText5237191281102806778" style="display:none">The name Poe brings to mind images of murderers and madmen, premature burials, and mysterious women who return from the dead. His works have been in print since 1827 and include such literary classics as <i>The Tell-Tale Heart</i>, <i>The Raven</i>, and The Fall of the House of Usher. This versatile writer’s oeuvre includes short stories, poetry, a novel, a textbook, a book of scientific theory, and hundreds of essays and book reviews. He is widely acknowledged as the inventor of the modern detective story and an innovator in the science fiction genre, but he made his living as America’s first great literary critic and theoretician. Poe’s reputation today rests primarily on his tales of terror as well as on his haunting lyric poetry.<br /><br />Just as the bizarre characters in Poe’s stories have captured the public imagination so too has Poe himself. He is seen as a morbid, mysterious figure lurking in the shadows of moonlit cemeteries or crumbling castles. This is the Poe of legend. But much of what we know about Poe is wrong, the product of a biography written by one of his enemies in an attempt to defame the author’s name.<br /><br />The real Poe was born to traveling actors in Boston on January 19, 1809. Edgar was the second of three children. His other brother William Henry Leonard Poe would also become a poet before his early death, and Poe’s sister Rosalie Poe would grow up to teach penmanship at a Richmond girls’ school. Within three years of Poe’s birth both of his parents had died, and he was taken in by the wealthy tobacco merchant John Allan and his wife Frances Valentine Allan in Richmond, Virginia while Poe’s siblings went to live with other families. Mr. Allan would rear Poe to be a businessman and a Virginia gentleman, but Poe had dreams of being a writer in emulation of his childhood hero the British poet Lord Byron. Early poetic verses found written in a young Poe’s handwriting on the backs of Allan’s ledger sheets reveal how little interest Poe had in the tobacco business.<br /><br />For more information, please see <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_allan_poe">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edgar_al...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="5237191281102806778" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
